        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="c7cc4f4bbbaa4524a2521972db1bbe34"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="c7cc4f4bbbaa4524a2521972db1bbe34">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '805a1e94-4362-47cf-ab35-5d1af3b43daa'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d38765243" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>While cost-effective sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with crystalline silicon anodes promise high theoretical capacities, they perform poorly because silicon stores sodium ineffectively (capacity &lt;40 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). To address this issue, herein an atomic-order structural-design tactic is adopted for obtaining unique multilevel gradient-ordered silicon (MGO-Si) by simple electrochemical reconstruction. In situ-formed short-range-, medium-range-, and long-range-ordered structures construct a stable MGO-Si, which contributes to favorable Na–Si interaction and fast ion diffusion channels. These characteristics afford a high reversible capacity (352.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>) and stable cycling performance (95.2% capacity retention after 4000 cycles), exhibiting record values among those reported for pure silicon electrodes. Sodium storage of MGO-Si involves an adsorption–intercalation mechanism, and a stepwise construction strategy of gradient-ordered structure further improves the specific capacity (339.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>). Reconstructed Si/C composites show a high reversible capacity of 449.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, significantly better than most carbonaceous anodes. The universality of this design principle is demonstrated for other inert or low-capacity materials (micro-Si, SiO<sub>2</sub>, SiC, graphite, and TiO<sub>2</sub>), boosting their capacities by 1.5–6 times that of pristine materials, thereby providing new solutions to facilitate sodium storage capability for better-performing battery designs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202310270-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202310270-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), which are cost-effective and environmentally sustainable, are emerging in the grid-scale energy storage market.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0001_R_d38765170e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#adma202310270-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0005_R_d38765170e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The remarkable promise of Na-based energy storage has placed significant emphasis on the development of high-performance electrode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0006_R_d38765170e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Commun.">6</a>-<a href="#adma202310270-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0008_R_d38765170e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Commun.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In terms of anode materials, while hard carbon is the most promising anode material for industrialization, its capacity is limited and presents a bottleneck for further development. Therefore, providing additional SIB-anode choices urgently requires the development of novel promising materials. Silicon, which has been widely used as an electrode in commercial lithium-ion batteries owing to its ultrahigh capacity and cost-effectiveness, also has a high theoretical capacity (954 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0009_R_d38765170e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Alloys Compd.">9</a>, <a href="#adma202310270-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0010_R_d38765170e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Predictably, the use of Si-based SIBs will create new opportunities for the development of Na-based energy storage. Unfortunately, silicon delivers negligible experimental capacity (&lt;40 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) in SIBs. Hence, Si was considered electrochemically “inactive” for a long time because crystalline Si was regarded as unsuitable for alloying with Na atoms;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0011_R_d38765170e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> this inactive nature of Si is believed to originate from positive binding energy of Na in bulk Si (+0.6&nbsp;eV),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0012_R_d38765170e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> low thermodynamic driving force (≈0.1&nbsp;eV),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0013_R_d38765170e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and high diffusion barrier (3.12&nbsp;eV in a Si nanosheet),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0014_R_d38765170e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Energy">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which indicate that Na insertion into Si is energetically infeasible underrealistic battery operating conditions. Although some nanoengineering,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0015_R_d38765170e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> carbon-coating,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0016_R_d38765170e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Electrochem. Commun.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and porous-structure<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0017_R_d38765170e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nanoscale">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> strategies promote Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and storage, limited progress has been reported because such efforts still hardly solve&nbsp;intrinsically unfavorable Na–Si interactions and sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the Si lattice.</p>
         
         <p>In essence, Na-storage chemistry is realized via interactions or electrochemical reactions involving Na<sup>+</sup> and the atoms of the host material. Therefore, the atomic structure and ordering of the host material greatly affect Na–host-material interactions, and consequently, the Na-storage performance. For instance, graphite, a long-range-ordered crystalline carbon material, was also considered inactive toward Na storage due to its small layer spacing and its inability to form stable binary Na–C compounds, such as NaC<sub>6</sub> and NaC<sub>8</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0018_R_d38765170e532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Solid State Ionics">18</a>-<a href="#adma202310270-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0021_R_d38765170e532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Phys. Chem. C">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By contrast, hard carbon, a short-range-ordered amorphous carbon material, realizes reversible Na storage through the mechanisms of Na intercalation into small short-range ordered graphitic layers, Na adsorption in defects, and Na filling in nanopores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0022_R_d38765170e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Energy Lett.">22</a>-<a href="#adma202310270-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0024_R_d38765170e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, expanded graphite, which is more ordered than hard carbon, delivers a favorable Na-storage capacity because its large layer spacing facilitates Na diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0025_R_d38765170e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Commun.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, we conclude that the electrochemical activeness of the host atoms is influenced by structural order and ion diffusion channels. Since silicon and carbon belong to the same main group, we infer that ordered structure regulation is also a useful method for boosting Na storage in silicon. Noteworthily, different ordered structures exhibit both merits and challenges. Although a short-range-ordered amorphous structure provides more diffusion channels and active sites for Na storage, abundant defects and dangling bonds lead to a large irreversible capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0026_R_d38765170e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A medium-ordered expanded material also faces challenges associated with high irreversibility owing to irreversible interactions between Na and other heteroatoms in the interlayer space.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0025_R_d38765170e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Commun.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, constructing expanded lattice in silicon has hardly been reported and achieved. While long-range-ordered crystalline materials may facilitate highly reversible Na storage and stable cycling, their capacities are restricted by limited active sites and narrow diffusion channels. Therefore, how to design and construct an optimally ordered silicon structure that delivers both high capacity and stable cycling remains challenging.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, inspired by the effects of ordered structures in conventional carbon materials, we report an unprecedented and extraordinary multilevel gradient-ordered silicon (MGO-Si) anode for high-performance Na storage. MGO-Si is composed of an outer short-range-ordered amorphous phase, a medium-range-ordered expanded lattice, and an inner long-range-ordered crystalline phase. This nontrivial structure was constructed using an innovative and simple electrochemical reconstruction method by virtue of the Na<sup>+</sup> implanting effect. MGO-Si delivered a high reversible capacity (352.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> after 120 cycles) and stable cycling performance (95.2% capacity retention after 4000 cycles at 500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), which are record-high values among those reported for a pure silicon anode in SIBs. The MGO-Si sodium-storage mechanism is shown to involve adsorption–intercalation behavior by the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), X-ray diffractometry (XRD), transmission electron microscopy (TEM), and scanning-TEM–electron-energy-loss spectroscopy (STEM–EELS). Importantly, the sodium-storage capacity is further improved by the stepwise construction of the gradient-ordered structure. More surprisingly, the electrode design principle is universal and boosts sodium-storage capacity in conventional inert materials (micro-Si, SiO<sub>2</sub>, SiC) and low-capacity anodes (graphite and TiO<sub>2</sub>). This design strategy provides concept and approach breakthroughs for developing high-performance SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202310270-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202310270-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310270-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202310270-sec-0030-title">2.1 Design Principle and Simulations</h3>
            
            <p>Current crystalline silicon materials suffer from negligible capacities when used in SIBs due to the high formation energies of Na–Si alloys and the high Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier in the silicon lattice. We aimed to modify the long-range-ordered crystal structure of silicon to medium-range-ordered expanded structure and a short-range-ordered amorphous structure to facilitate ion diffusion. The expanded and amorphous Si structures were formed by a high-rate electrochemical reconstruction process, which is inspired by ion implantation in semiconductor materials. Notably, the ion-insertion dosage during one discharge process is even higher than that of conventional ion implantation (10<sup>18</sup>&nbsp;vs 10<sup>16</sup> atom cm<sup>−2</sup>, according to the calculations in Note <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2.1</a> in the Supporting Information). Consequently, the prompt transfer of sodium ions during the electrochemical reconstruction process induces the implantation of Na<sup>+</sup> into the silicon material. Specifically, Na<sup>+</sup> implantation induces partial amorphization via atomic dislocation on the crystal surface, which introduces additional defects and diffusion channels for Na<sup>+</sup> that improve sodium-storage capacity. Some Na remains trapped in the Si matrix following reconstruction, because Na has a limited diffusion capability and cannot completely diffuse from the matrix, which leads to the formation of expanded silicon. Lattice expansion results in longer Si─Si bonds and larger local volumes occupied by Na atoms, which is beneficial for Na diffusion. Additionally, because presodiation introduces large amounts of Na<sup>+</sup> in the expanded lattice, Na diffusion is further enhanced as it is similar to self-diffusion since Na atoms experience fewer interactions with Si atoms. Consequently, a gradient-ordered structure composed of a superficial amorphous structure, a transitional expanded lattice, and an inner crystalline core is obtained (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202310270-fig-0001">1a</a>). Moreover, since both the amorphous and expanded Si phases are in situ formed on the original crystalline silicon, there are no obvious phase boundaries between the various microstructures, thereby avoiding sluggish Na diffusion between different phase interfaces, which benefits fast sodium migration.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310270-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9cea3d0b-628e-4684-a508-a18fa09901f2/adma202310270-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9cea3d0b-628e-4684-a508-a18fa09901f2/adma202310270-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a05e4950-ace4-4fb8-8375-56dbe1998f5a/adma202310270-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9cea3d0b-628e-4684-a508-a18fa09901f2/adma202310270-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310270-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310270" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Effect of atomic arrangement on the silicon anode. a) Schematic of multilevel gradient-ordered silicon. b) Predicted pathways, c) activation barriers (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) for Na diffusion, and d) DOSs for c-Si, 5%-e-Si, 10%-e-Si, and 20%-e-Si. e) Atomic structure of amorphous Si. f) Na-binding energies (Δ<i>E</i><sub>b</sub>) for c-Si, 5%-e-Si, 10%-e-Si, 20%-e-Si, and a-Si.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To predict and understand whether expanded Si affects sodium storage in a positive way, migration pathways and activation energies (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) for Na diffusion were simulated for expanded Si with various expansion ratios (0%, 5%, 10%, and 20%), which are referred to as: c-Si, 5%-e-Si, 10%-e-Si, and 20%-e-Si. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0001">1b,c</a> shows that Na migration is predicted to occur through jumping between adjacent T-sites via hexagonal-sites. The smallest <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> value (0.10&nbsp;eV), and therefore, the lowest diffusion barrier, was calculated for 20%-e-Si, which is attributable to effective diffusion channels and lower rigidity in the expanded silicon lattice. Moreover, Na–Si interactions and the electronic structures of the various expanded silicon species are revealed by their densities of states (DOSs). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) reveals that 20%-e-Si shows the narrowest Si peak, which is ascribable to its weak Si─Si bonds. Additionally, the Na and Si states in c-Si and 5%-e-Si substantially overlap, consistent with a high degree of covalency. The less-obvious overlap observed for 20%-e-Si is indicative of more ionic Na–Si interactions, implying a more-favorable Na–Si reaction. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0001">1d</a> shows that 20%-e-Si has the smallest bandgap, which is attributable to electronic redistribution in the expanded lattice owing to Na<sup>+</sup>-implantation-induced breakdown that facilitates fast electron transfer during electrochemical reactions, which is similar to reported work.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0027_R_d38765170e662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To confirm that the expanded Si exhibits excellent Na-storage behavior, we further compared Na–Si interactions in expanded Si with those of amorphous Si (a-Si) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0001">1e,f</a>), which is reported to favor Na storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0028_R_d38765170e671" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among the modeled materials, 20%-e-Si and a-Si show negative Na-binding energies, which implies that Na insertion into these two materials is spontaneous and favorable; 20%-e-Si is expected to insert Na most easily due to the lowest energy value. Further calculational details are provided in Note <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2.2</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Therefore, compared to the amorphous and crystalline silicon structures, expanded Si with the optimized electronic structure shows great potential for use in advanced anodes in SIBs, consistent with our design concept.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310270-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202310270-sec-0040-title">2.2 Process Optimization and Structural Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>A pristine crystalline Si electrode and Na metal were used to assemble a primary half-cell. Pristine crystalline Si undergoes electrochemical reconstruction in the half-cell with given discharge/charge cycles and high current density. The electrochemical reconstruction can be regarded as activation process. The optimal activating current density and number of activating cycles were determined (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202310270-fig-0002">2a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information)). Notably, 30 A g<sup>−1</sup> is the highest current density sustainable by the silicon electrode; as a result, all activated samples show significantly higher capacities than pristine crystalline Si (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The optimal gradient-ordered silicon electrode activated at 30 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 500 cycles (MGO-Si) exhibits balanced performance, with high capacity and stable cycling (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0002">2a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information)), indicating abundant sodium-storage sites and fast ion diffusion. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and Raman spectroscopy (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0002">2b</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4–S6</a> (Supporting Information)) reveal that insufficient activation (&lt;500 cycles) leads to insufficient atomic structural change, a relatively low degree of amorphization (calculational details are provided in Note <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2.3</a> and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> in the Supporting Information), and limited capacity, while excessive structural evolution (&gt;500 cycles) results in particle rupturing, structural collapse, and inferior cycling stability. The MGO-Si electrode shows a high degree of amorphization and expansion (62.0%, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0002">2i</a>) and a stable structure. As concluded, both the short-range-ordered amorphous phase and the remaining long-range-ordered crystalline structure benefit MGO-Si, since the former provides active sites that promote sodium storage, while the latter acts as an inactive substrate that stabilizes the structural integrity of each silicon particle.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310270-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/28b6ede0-a3af-42b3-a332-28225cf2031c/adma202310270-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/28b6ede0-a3af-42b3-a332-28225cf2031c/adma202310270-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/05e1cef6-4faf-484a-90f1-62d574a2d165/adma202310270-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/28b6ede0-a3af-42b3-a332-28225cf2031c/adma202310270-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310270-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310270" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterizing the structures of pristine c-Si and MGO-Si and optimizing the activation process. a) Initial capacities (blue dots) and capacity retention rates (red dots), and b) amorphization degrees of pristine crystalline Si and MGO-Si at various activating current densities (blue) and numbers of activating cycles (red). c) TEM image, d) HR-TEM image and SAED pattern (inset), and e) enlarged filtered TEM image of pristine crystalline Si. f) TEM image, g) HR-TEM image and SAED pattern (inset), and h) enlarged filtered TEM image of MGO-Si. i) Raman spectrum of pristine crystalline Si (top) and MGO-Si (bottom). j) XRD patterns and enlarged diffraction peaks of pristine crystalline Si (blue) and MGO-Si (red). k) Rocking curves for pristine crystalline Si (blue) and MGO-Si (red). RSM images of l) pristine crystalline Si and m) MGO-Si.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The structures of pristine crystalline Si and MGO-Si were further characterized to understand the structural evolution that takes place during the electrochemical reconstruction process. SEM (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information) and TEM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0002">2c</a>) reveal that pristine crystalline Si has a perfect nanospherical morphology, with particles that are 89.59&nbsp;nm in size (on average); its surface is smooth and devoid of obvious aggregation. High-resolution TEM (HR-TEM) and the corresponding fast Fourier-transform image show the long-range-ordered crystalline nature of pristine Si, as evidenced by the point lattice set in the inset of the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0002">2d</a>) and well-resolved lattice fringes that show an average spacing of 0.31&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0002">2e</a>). In comparison, the strain induced by Na<sup>+</sup> implantation is mainly released near the surface following electrochemical reconstruction; hence, the MGO-Si surface is partially damaged, resulting in irregular spherical structures, while the ideal crystalline structure is retained away from the surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0002">2f</a>). MGO-Si exhibits an obvious multilevel structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0002">2g,h</a>); the outer surface shows distinctly disordered amorphous characteristics, while the inner core particles are completely crystalline, with an average lattice spacing of 0.31&nbsp;nm. Notably, a medium-range-ordered expanded lattice structure with a local 1–5&nbsp;nm range is evidently formed in the middle region between the amorphous and crystalline phases (highlighted in blue in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0002">2g,h</a>), where distorted lattice fringes show an enlarged average lattice distance of 0.37&nbsp;nm, which shows an expansion ratio of 19.35%, corresponding to the theoretical results. The coexistence of disordered and ordered structures in MGO-Si is also supported by an indistinct diffraction ring with wide halos and the remaining point lattice in the inset of the SAED pattern. Taken together, these results show that the three different ordered structures are tightly coupled without any obvious phase boundary. Additionally, expanded Si formation was further confirmed by XRD. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0002">2j</a> shows that MGO-Si exhibits diffraction peaks at lower angle than those of pristine crystalline silicon after activation, which implies the enlarged lattice spacing in MGO-Si according to Bragg's Law. Noteworthily, MGO-Si shows the same number of diffraction peaks as pristine crystalline silicon, which implies that no new Si–Na phases are formed during activation. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0002">2k</a> shows that the MGO-Si rocking curve has a wider full width at half maximum (FWHM) than that of pristine crystalline silicon (1.07&nbsp;vs 0.55). The broader FWHM of MGO-Si can be attributed to the higher dislocation concentration, which is corresponding to the formation of amorphous Si and expanded Si. Moreover, reciprocal space mapping (RSM) is a high-resolution X-ray technique for analyzing crystallinity, lattice spacing variations, and stress distributions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0029_R_d38765170e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Crit. Rev. Solid State Mater. Sci.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0002">2l</a> shows a RSM image of the (111) face of pristine crystalline silicon, which exhibits a defined fringe and a central maximum. In comparison, the RSM image of the (111) face of MGO-Si (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0002">2m</a>) shows a less well-defined Bragg peak, with a hard-to-identify clear central maximum and a significantly lower data-volume value that corresponds to the larger crystal-lattice spacing in MGO-Si, consistent with previous reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0030_R_d38765170e800" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Struct. Dyn.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310270-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202310270-sec-0050-title">2.3 Na-Storage Performance</h3>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202310270-fig-0003">3a</a> shows that the designed MGO-Si electrode exhibits a high initial charge capacity of 950.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, which significantly exceeds the initial discharge capacity, indicating high initial Coulombic efficiency. The extraction of preinserted Na<sup>+</sup> contributes to the ultrahigh charge capacity of the MGO-Si electrode. Moreover, the high Coulombic efficiency can be illustrated by the preformation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) during the electrochemical reconstruction process. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), after electrochemical reconstruction, the preformed SEI is obviously observed in the surface of MGO-Si in the Scanning TEM-high angle annular dark field (STEM-HADDF) and HR-TEM images of cryo-TEM. The charge capacity gradually decreases during the first 20 cycles due to decreasing amounts of preinserted Na<sup>+</sup>. Noteworthily, extracting the preinserted Na<sup>+</sup> has little effect on the as-formed expanded lattice structure because the discharge capacity is stably maintained during the entire 120 cycles, consistent with stable and reversible sodium storage in the gradient-ordered silicon electrode. The MGO-Si electrode exhibits a high reversible capacity of 352.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 120 cycles, which is a record-high capacity for a pure silicon electrode in SIBs (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the MGO-Si electrode shows excellent cycling stability, with 92.5% of the capacity retained after 100 cycles (using the 20th cycle as a reference). Even with high mass loading of 4&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, the MGO-Si shows a high reversible capacity of 117.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles, with a high capacity retention ratio of 93.9%, demonstrating that the MGO-Si is suitable for practical use in SIBs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The superior capacity and cycling stability are ascribable to the structural stability of MGO-Si during cycling. In comparison, pristine crystalline Si delivers a negligible capacity of 28.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which is ≈12 times smaller than that of the MGO-Si anode. The discharged voltage profile of MGO-Si at the 20th cycle exhibits two sloping voltage plateaus at 0.7 and 0.2&nbsp;V, indicating Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into the MGO-Si electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310270-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0b702c4a-6f5c-4bc5-8805-217dec2fa177/adma202310270-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0b702c4a-6f5c-4bc5-8805-217dec2fa177/adma202310270-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2149e18c-778b-4c4d-bae4-e8ad1ccf955b/adma202310270-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0b702c4a-6f5c-4bc5-8805-217dec2fa177/adma202310270-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310270-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310270" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical sodium-storage performance of MGO-Si and crystalline Si. a) Cycling performance of MGO-Si and pristine crystalline Si at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. b) Comparing rate performance. c) Charge/discharge curves for the MGO-Si electrode at various rates. d) Long-term cycling stability of MGO-Si at 500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Specific capacities at low rates, final capacities, specific capacities at high rates, cycle numbers, and capacity retentions of reported silicon electrodes (Si nanoparticles,<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0010" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0010_R_d38765170e878" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> c-Si/ai-Si nanowires,<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0032" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0032_R_d38765170e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> a-Si nanomembranes,<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0030" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0030_R_d38765170e889" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Struct. Dyn.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Si/C nanofibers<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0031" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0031_R_d38765170e894" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>) in SIBs compared with the MGO-Si electrode in this work. f) Charge/discharge curves for the P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> | MGO-Si full cell at 300&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. g) Full cell cycling performance at 0.3 A g<sup>−1</sup> in the 1.0–3.6&nbsp;V voltage range.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The rate capability of the designed MGO-Si electrode was also evaluated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0003">3b,c</a>). MGO-Si shows capacities of 247.9, 175.6, 131.2, 108.1, and 91.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which are much higher than those of the pristine crystalline Si electrode. The excellent rate performance of the MGO-Si electrode is related to its rapid sodium-storage kinetics, as evidenced by cyclic voltammetry (CV) at various scan rates, and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the long-term cycling stability of MGO-Si was evaluated at 500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0003">3d</a>); it exhibits a high capacity of 178.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 4000 cycles, with a high capacity retention of 95.2%, which represents record ultralong cycling stability for a pure silicon anode in SIBs and is to be compared with the performance of amorphous silicon nanomembranes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0030_R_d38765170e936" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Struct. Dyn.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Si/C,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0031_R_d38765170e942" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and core/shell-structured silicon nanowires<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0032_R_d38765170e947" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0003">3e</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information)). Moreover, to examine the practical applicability of the MGO-Si electrode, for the first time, we assembled a full cell using MGO-Si as the anode and P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as the cathode in an ether-based electrolyte. The electrochemical performance of P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode is provided in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information). The full cell delivered a high discharge capacity of 140.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (based on the anode mass) with an average operating voltage of 2.7&nbsp;V when tested at 0.3 A g<sup>−1</sup> in the 1−3.6&nbsp;V voltage range (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0003">3f,g</a>). Additionally, the full cell also exhibits favorable cycling stability with a low capacity decay rate of 0.2% per cycle over 150 cycles, which highlights the outstanding electrochemical applicability of the MGO-Si anode for SIBs.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310270-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202310270-sec-0060-title">2.4 Adsorption–Intercalation Na-Storage Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>Currently, the alloyin mechanism,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0031_R_d38765170e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> solid-solution sodiation/desodiation reaction,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0010_R_d38765170e1000" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the surface absorption mechanism<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0026_R_d38765170e1005" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been proposed as Na-storage mechanisms for the silicon anode; however, the actual storage mechanism remains controversial because of a lack of direct evidence. The sodiation–desodiation behavior of MGO-Si was disclosed by CV experiments. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information), the MGO-Si electrode exhibits three pairs of redox peaks, with cathodic peaks at 0.8, 0.2, and 0.03&nbsp;V. The broad and sharp peaks at ≈0.8 and ≈0.03&nbsp;V are believed to be attributed to Na uptake in amorphous and crystalline silicon, respectively, in agreement with previous works.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0010_R_d38765170e1014" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#adma202310270-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0026_R_d38765170e1014" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cathodic peak at 0.2&nbsp;V is possibly attributed to Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into expanded Si. GITT was used to further understand the sodium-storage behavior of the MGO-Si electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). The sloping voltage platform at &gt;0.3&nbsp;V indicates the formation of a single-phase Si–Na solid solution and the surface adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> in amorphous Si,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0033_R_d38765170e1027" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ChemSusChem">33</a>, <a href="#adma202310270-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0034_R_d38765170e1027" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nano Lett.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while the voltage platform at &lt;0.3&nbsp;V is due to the intercalation of Na into expanded Si, consistent with the CV data.</p>
            
            <p>XRD analysis of MGO-Si at various cut-off voltages was conducted to elucidate the roles of the expanded and amorphous Si phases in the sodium-storage process (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202310270-fig-0004">4a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information)) by characterizing structural changes during discharging and charging. The (111) diffraction peak of MGO-Si shifts to a lower angle during discharging and back again during charging, consistent with increases and decreases in the lattice distance associated with sodiation and desodiation, respectively, which strongly manifests the Na intercalation mechanism in expanded Si. The interstitial T-sites (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0001">1b</a>) of expanded Si are large enough to allow the occupation of inserted Na<sup>+</sup> with little crystal distortion. The lattice strain upon full intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> is not significant (generally less than 1%), which is consistent with previous reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0010_R_d38765170e1052" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#adma202310270-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0034_R_d38765170e1052" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nano Lett.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The slight lattice strain also benefits the well structural stability of expanded Si during cycling. Additionally, no extra peak is observed during sodiation/desodiation; hence, no crystalline alloying compounds are formed when Si reacts with Na. In addition, the value of the MGO-Si(111) diffraction peak is larger after cycling than activated state, which is ascribable to extraction of preinserted Na in the expanded lattice during the charging process in the initial cycle.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310270-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/01e6b109-820d-406e-bfbf-8f0176c1a8db/adma202310270-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/01e6b109-820d-406e-bfbf-8f0176c1a8db/adma202310270-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7ad29d7a-a2c4-40de-8ff7-a11cc2bbb718/adma202310270-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/01e6b109-820d-406e-bfbf-8f0176c1a8db/adma202310270-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310270-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310270" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Investigating the reaction mechanism of the MGO-Si electrode. a) Contour plot of ex situ XRD patterns of the (111) and (200) MGO-Si peak at various cut-off voltages during the discharge–charge process. b) Ex situ TEM images and c) enlarged filtered HR-TEM images of MGO-Si before cycling and when discharged and charged. d,e) STEM–EELS line scan and the corresponding EELS spectra of sodiated MGO-Si. f,g) TEM line scans of MGO-Si when discharged and charged.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To visualize its microstructural evolution, HR-TEM images of MGO-Si before cycling, at discharge state and charge state are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0004">4b</a>, with enlarged high-frequency-noise-filtered images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0004">4c</a> displaying the representative microstructural characteristics of MGO-Si by extracting relevant contrast. The MGO-Si microstructure is composed of crystalline Si with a slightly deformed lattice in the core, and expanded Si with a larger lattice distance near the surface. Lattice disorder is more convoluted and distorted, with ripple-like characteristics observed after discharging, which is attributable to morphological change that occurs during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into MGO-Si. The morphology of the desodiated MGO-Si lattice is reversibly recovered and exhibits a slightly distorted pattern after charging due to Na<sup>+</sup> extraction from lattice matrix. These lattice changes are consistent with an intercalation mechanism in the expanded Si. Notably, no obvious microstructural distortion or disturbance is observed during cycling, indicating well-maintained microstructural stability that contributes to stable MGO-Si electrode cycling.</p>
            
            <p>Since the position of the plasmon peak in the low-energy-loss region is strongly related to valence and the chemical bonding state,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0035_R_d38765170e1092" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nat. Energy">35</a>-<a href="#adma202310270-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0037_R_d38765170e1092" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Energy Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> STEM–EELS line scanning (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0004">4d,e</a>) and spectral imaging in the plasmon energy range was used to understand the charge-storage behavior of MGO-Si. According to reference spectra,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0038_R_d38765170e1100" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">38</a>, <a href="#adma202310270-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0039_R_d38765170e1100" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nano Lett.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the plasmon energy signal of MGO-Si in state 1 exhibits the features of standard Si, which indicates that the inner part of MGO-Si contains crystalline Si that hardly reacts with the inserted Na<sup>+</sup>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0004">4e</a> shows that the energy of the silicon plasmon peak decreases in moving from state 1 to state 5 (interior to surface), consistent with increasing degrees of sodiation, which is similar to the lithiation of silicon anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0039_R_d38765170e1111" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nano Lett.">39</a>, <a href="#adma202310270-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0040_R_d38765170e1111" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, a STEM image and mapping of discharged MGO-Si are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17a,b</a> (Supporting Information) with crystalline and amorphous Si regions (identified by multiple linear least-squares energy-loss fitting of the Si-L edge near-edge structure) shown in red and green, respectively. Evidently, the inner Si maintains its crystallinity following Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into MGO-Si. Therefore, Na<sup>+</sup> is mainly stored in the expanded and amorphous Si near the surface rather than in the inner crystalline phase during discharging, as further evidenced by the TEM line scan of discharged MGO-Si (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0004">4f</a>), which shows that Na mainly exists in the expanded and amorphous Si near the surface (highlighted in yellow). Most of the Na is extracted from MGO-Si during charging, while some Na<sup>+</sup> preinserted during activation remains in the expanded Si region (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0004">4g</a>).</p>
            
            <p>Moreover, we examined the electrochemical performance of commercial amorphous Si to clarify whether the inserted Na<sup>+</sup> is mainly stored in the expanded or amorphous Si region (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information) shows that activated commercial amorphous Si only delivers a poor capacity of 47.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, which is significantly improved to 306.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> following electrochemical reconstruction, strongly suggesting that intercalation in expanded Si mainly contributes to capacity of MGO-Si.</p>
            
            <p>Overall, the above discussion clearly demonstrates an adsorption–intercalation Na-storage mechanism in the MGO-Si electrode. Short-range-ordered amorphous Si adsorbs Na<sup>+</sup> through its abundant defects and dangling bonds, while medium-range-ordered expanded Si provides sufficient lattice space for Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation, and inner long-range-ordered crystalline Si stabilizes the structure. Remarkably, the expanded Si plays a more important Na-storage role than amorphous Si.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310270-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202310270-sec-0070-title">2.5 Stepwise Construction of the Gradient-Ordered Structure</h3>
            
            <p>While the inner crystalline Si stabilizes the electrode structure, its sodium-storage inactivity lowers the overall capacity of the MGO-Si electrode; therefore, regulating the proportion of crystalline Si is important in order to balance specific capacity against structural stability. However, as mentioned above, single activation using a large number of cycles results in a damaged structure. To address this issue, we contrived a unique stepwise electrochemical reconstruction that enables stable Na storage in the MGO-Si electrode. Specifically, to prevent structural collapse, a single 200-cycle activation process was performed to construct gradient-order structure and realized gradual conversion from the crystalline Si to expanded Si and amorphous Si in steps. This stepwise construction process is found to further improve the capacity and cycling stability of the MGO-Si electrode. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202310270-fig-0005">5a</a> shows that the reversible capacity of MGO-Si is gradually improved, from 217.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (after the first activation step) to 286.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (after the second activation step), with a high reversible capacity of 339.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after the third activation step. However, while an even higher initial capacity (409.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) is observed after a fourth step, it decays rapidly over 20 cycles, which is attributed to structural damage and the loss of electrical contact between active materials. The surge in Coulombic efficiency observed during the initial cycle of each activation step is ascribed to the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> newly inserted in each activation step.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310270-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8ac9b280-52a9-4eed-87f5-b743471e1c77/adma202310270-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8ac9b280-52a9-4eed-87f5-b743471e1c77/adma202310270-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/24d9b405-60e3-4e6e-ac41-d1a9122f646a/adma202310270-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8ac9b280-52a9-4eed-87f5-b743471e1c77/adma202310270-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310270-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310270" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Stepwise MGO-Si reconstruction. a) Cycling performance of MGO-Si after the first, second, third, and fourth activation steps at a current density of 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. b) TEM and c) HR-TEM images of MGO-Si after the second activation step. d) TEM and e) HR-TEM images of MGO-Si after the third activation step. f) TEM image of MGO-Si after the fourth activation step. g) XRD patterns of MGO-Si before and after each activation step.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>TEM was used to gain further insight into the structural evolution of the MGO-Si electrode during stepwise activation. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0005">5b–f</a> shows that the active-material particles gradually become smaller, with a spherical-to-irregular change in morphology, which is attributed to particle cleavage induced by the movements of large numbers of dislocations and defects during each activation step. The smaller particles contribute to a higher specific surface area and the formation of expanded and amorphous Si phases near the MGO-Si surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0005">5c,e</a>). The larger expanded and amorphous Si regions account for the observed significant improvement in Na-storage capacity. Unfortunately, the silicon particles are severely cracked and aggregated after the fourth activation step (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0005">5f</a>) leading to significant capacity degradation. In addition, the structural evolution of the MGO-Si electrode during stepwise activation was investigated by XRD. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0005">5g</a> shows that the (111) MGO-Si diffraction peak is generally shifted to a smaller angle following each activation step, consistent with an enlarged lattice distance ascribed to the formation of additional expanded Si. Moreover, this diffraction peak is observed to return to higher angle following cycling, consistent with the extraction of preinserted Na<sup>+</sup> from the expanded Si and a lower amount of expanded Si, which is responsible for the initial capacity loss. Overall, the unique stepwise activation method is essential to enable the sustainability of the MGO-Si anodes for Na storage by the simultaneous optimization of the regenerated expanded/amorphous Si and the rational occupation of the intrinsic crystalline Si.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310270-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202310270-sec-0080-title">2.6 Applicability to Other Anodes</h3>
            
            <p>To investigate the applicability of electrochemical reconstruction method, Si/C composite material was obtained by a simple high-energy ball-milling strategy due to the effects of carbon in improving electrical conductivity and buffering Si volume changes. After the electrochemical reconstruction of 30 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 500 cycles, the reconstructed Si/C delivered a high specific capacity of 449.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20a,b</a>, Supporting Information), which is significantly better than most of carbonaceous anodes. Based on the novel concept and strategy in this work, the sodium storage performance of Si-based electrodes is on a par with carbonaceous anode, providing new opportunities and potentials for SIBs.</p>
            
            <p>To further demonstrate the universality of our design principle, we investigated the abilities of other intrinsically inactive or less-active materials (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202310270-fig-0006">6a</a>) to store Na after the same electrochemical reconstruction tactic. Microcrystalline Si (micro-Si), SiO<sub>2</sub>, and SiC are rarely reported as active materials for SIBs. Surprisingly, the reconstructed micro-Si electrode displays a high initial capacity of 469.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and reversibly maintains 206.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles, which is almost 6 times higher than that of the pristine micro-Si electrode (34.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21a,b</a>, Supporting Information). Similarly, the reconstructed SiO<sub>2</sub> and SiC electrodes also exhibit significantly improved Na-storage capacities of 147.5 and 180.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which are much better than those of the SiO<sub>2</sub> and SiC electrodes (&lt;50 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0006">6b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> (Supporting Information)). To understand the factors responsible for this performance improvement, we investigated the structural evolution of the micro-Si electrode (as an example) during activation. The crystalline nature and blocky morphology of micro-Si are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a> (Supporting Information). Bulk micro-Si is pulverized into nanometer-sized particles that then form a 3D porous coral-like structure during activation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24a,b</a>, Supporting Information). This structure shows gradient-ordered character and is composed of obvious amorphous Si, expanded Si, and crystalline Si phases (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24c</a>, Supporting Information), in which the amorphous and expanded Si are responsible for enhanced Na storage.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310270-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/17682353-206f-481b-b2ab-0567f6838b70/adma202310270-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/17682353-206f-481b-b2ab-0567f6838b70/adma202310270-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f8bb535b-0a7e-4c1d-b1a1-d78572177f6f/adma202310270-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/17682353-206f-481b-b2ab-0567f6838b70/adma202310270-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310270-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310270" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>

J.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> N doped carbon nanosheet-TiO<sub>2</sub><sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adma202310270-bib-0060" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310270-bib-0060_R_d38765170e1453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Adv. Energy Mater.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Some less-active materials, such as graphite and TiO<sub>2</sub>, were also investigated. Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a> (Supporting Information) show that a significantly medium-range-ordered expanded lattice and a short-range-ordered amorphous structure are generated in the reconstructed graphite and TiO<sub>2</sub>, respectively. The three reconstructed electrodes all show galvanostatic charge–discharge curves with obviously smaller slopes and smaller platforms below 0.3&nbsp;V compared to those of the pristine electrodes, indicative of Na adsorption in the amorphous phase and Na insertion into the expanded lattice, respectively. The reconstructed graphite and TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes exhibit improved reversible Na-storage capacities of 223.4 and 314.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0006">6b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information)). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310270-fig-0006">6c</a> shows that the specific capacity of the reconstructed graphite is the highest recorded for a pure graphite anode in SIBs. The reconstructed TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode also shows significantly improved Na-storage performance compared to previously reported precisely designed TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes.</p>
            
            <p>Overall, these results clearly demonstrate the universal feasibility of our design principle, which offers a new pathway for boosting Na-storage performance. The commonly used anodes such as graphite, TiO<sub>2</sub>, the size-limited micro-Si, and even the never-reported anodes such as SiO<sub>2</sub> and SiC, can achieve significantly improved capacities by unique electrochemical activation strategy. This simple, effective, and energy-efficient tactic provides novel concept and breakthrough for exploiting and developing novel high-performance electrode materials, which can significantly contribute to the innovation of electrodes and wide use of applicable sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202310270-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202310270-sec-0090-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>Usually, Si is popular anode in LIBs, however, abandoned in SIBs because of poor capacity (&lt;40 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). Herein, Si is revived and shows activating sodium storage in SIBs by regulating atomic ordering structure. We first formed a gradient-ordered structure composed of a superficial short-range-ordered amorphous structure, a transitional medium-range-ordered expanded lattice, and an inner long-range-ordered crystalline core by atomic rearrangement induced by high-dose Na<sup>+</sup> implantation with a unique in situ electrochemical reconstruction. The amorphous phase adsorbs Na<sup>+</sup> owing to abundant dangling bonds, the expanded lattice shows favorable Na–Si interaction and provides rapid Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channels for Na intercalation, and the crystalline phase stabilizes the electrode structure. Therefore, the sodium storage is shown to involve an adsorption–intercalation mechanism, which is wholly different from alloying mechanism to form Li<sub>15</sub>Si<sub>4</sub> in lithium-ion batteries. Meantime, this combination of various ordered structures contributes to the high capacity and long cycling life of MGO-Si. The silicon anode delivers a record-high reversible capacity of 352.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and stable cycling performance, with 95.2% of its capacity retained after 4000 cycles. Additionally, the specific capacity is further improved to 339.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> by the stepwise construction of the gradient-ordered structure, which maintains a stable microstructure and achieves high-performance Na storage. The reconstructed Si/C composites shows a high reversible capacity of 449.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, significantly better than most of carbonaceous anodes. Intriguingly, the universal concept reported herein is shown to activate a number of inert (micro-Si, SiO<sub>2</sub>, and SiC) and less-active (graphite and TiO<sub>2</sub>) materials, increasing their Na-storage capacity by 1.5–6 times higher than pristine materials. Overall, our strategy provides conceptual, material, and technical breakthroughs for developing high-performance energy storage systems, for example, other secondary batteries besides SIBs, electrocatalysis systems, and even semiconductor devices.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
